        gptPage = window.digitalData && digitalData.page && digitalData.page.page ? digitalData.page.page : "blank";

        function gptClean(gptText) {
            if (typeof (gptText) == "string") {
                gptText = gptText.replace(/\&/g, '%26');
                return gptText.replace(/\^|\*|#|\$|\"|\'|\=|\,|\!|\+|\~|\;|\(|\)|\<|\>|\[|\]|\?|\%|\:|\@|\;|\&|\||\{|\}|\\|/g, '');
            }
            return "blank";
        }

            .setTargeting('property', 'value-of-property')    // Change and duplicate this line for as many additional keywords to send
            .setTargeting('doi', gptClean('10.1002/smll.201601530')) // Publication DOI
            .setTargeting('pbPage', gptClean(gptPage))
            .setTargeting('pbSubPage', gptClean(gptSubPage))
            .setTargeting('urlpath', gptClean(document.location.pathname));

        googletag.defineSlot($GooglePublisherCategory, 'fluid', 'advert-leaderboard')

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="db214f189c8f47b2a3a9ba9acc3abb96">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: 'f394198c-9b33-4735-a6cb-a6a84ec89ce7'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

        (function() {
            function riskifiedBeaconLoad() {
                var store_domain = 'https://onlinelibrary.wiley.com';
                var session_id = '7E822626B780A8605AD213B1AFA35CC5';
                var url = ('https:' == document.location.protocol ? 'https://' : 'http://')
                    + "beacon.riskified.com?shop=" + store_domain + "&sid=" + session_id;
                var s = document.createElement('script');
                s.type = 'text/javascript';
                s.async = true;
                s.src = url;
                var x = document.getElementsByTagName('script')[0];
                x.parentNode.insertBefore(s, x);
            }
            if (window.attachEvent)
                window.attachEvent('onload', riskifiedBeaconLoad)
            else
                window.addEventListener('load', riskifiedBeaconLoad, false);
        })();

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d145161494" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            <p>Sodium ion battery is one of the promising rechargeable batteries due to the low-cost and abundant sodium sources. In this work, a monolithic sodium ion battery based on a Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> cathode, MoS<sub>2</sub> layered anode, and polyether-based polymer electrolyte is reported. In addition, a new kind of polysulfonamide-supported poly(ethylene glycol) divinyl ether based polymer electrolyte is also demonstrated for monolithic sodium ion battery via in situ preparation. The resultant polymer electrolyte exhibits relatively high ionic conductivity (1.2 mS cm<sup>−1</sup>) at ambient temperature, wide electrochemical window (4.7 V), and favorable mechanical strength (25 MPa). Moreover, such a monolithic Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>/MoS<sub>2</sub> sodium ion battery using this polymer electrolyte delivers outstanding rate capability (up to 10 C) and superior cyclic stability (84%) after 1000 cycles at 0.5 C. What is more essential, such a polymer electrolyte based soft-package monolithic sodium ion cell can still power a red light emitting diode lamp and run finite times without suffering from any internal short-circuit failures, even in the case of a bended and wrinkled state. Considering these aspects, this work no doubt provides a new approach for the design of a high-performance polymer electrolyte toward monolithic sodium ion battery with exceptional rate capability and high safety.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll201601530-sec-0010">
         
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll201601530-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>At present, the extension of cost-effective battery technology for wide-scale implementation of renewable energy storage systems would be indispensable.<span><a href="#smll201601530-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201601530-bib-0001_R_d145161486e505" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Nature">1</a>-<a href="#smll201601530-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201601530-bib-0005_R_d145161486e505" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Adv. Mater.">5</a></span> Hence, sodium ion battery has received more attention as a novel and promising alternative to lithium ion battery due to the natural abundance of sodium resources and the similar intercalation chemistry of sodium and lithium.<span><a href="#smll201601530-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201601530-bib-0006_R_d145161486e507" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">6</a>-<a href="#smll201601530-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201601530-bib-0013_R_d145161486e507" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Energy Environ. Sci.">13</a></span> In general, sodium ion battery could be divided into four parts: cathode, anode, separator, and electrolyte. Currently, tremendous effort has been made to search for new positive electrode materials and negative electrode materials toward rechargeable sodium ion battery.<span><a href="#smll201601530-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201601530-bib-0014_R_d145161486e509" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Adv. Energy Mater.">14</a>-<a href="#smll201601530-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201601530-bib-0018_R_d145161486e509" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Nat. Commun.">18</a></span> Reliable and high-performance sodium ion batteries are desirable for consumer electronics, electrical vehicles, and grid energy storage. Safety issues have become a major obstacle impeding the sodium battery. Hence, besides implementation of suitable alternative electrodes, the investigation of electrolyte and separator is considered as a key step for developing sodium ion batteries with reliability and highly safety. Only should we balance the development between the electrode materials and separator electrolyte materials, the sodium ion battery industry could have a better future.</p>
         
         <p>Generally, the most widely used separators and electrolyte for sodium ion battery were polyolefin-based separators and liquid electrolyte (such as 1 <span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub> in EC/DMC).<span><a href="#smll201601530-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201601530-bib-0019_R_d145161486e518" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Chem. Commun.">19</a>-<a href="#smll201601530-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201601530-bib-0023_R_d145161486e518" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - J. Power Sources">23</a></span> However, such sodium batteries using liquid electrolyte and polyolefin-based separators might exhibit severe safety risk during cycling or storage, especially at elevated temperatures, resulting from the volatilization of the electrolyte. These problems finally could deteriorate the battery performance and become a potential constraining factor for practical utility of sodium ion battery.<span><a href="#smll201601530-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201601530-bib-0024_R_d145161486e520" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - J. Power Sources">24</a>-<a href="#smll201601530-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201601530-bib-0026_R_d145161486e520" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - J. Power Sources">26</a></span> One of the technologies is to develop polymer electrolyte with comprehensive performance in terms of high ionic conductivity, wide electrochemical window, and superior safety. Polymer electrolyte is undoubtedly an ideal solution due to their several advantages such as improved safety and reliability, compared to those with liquid electrolytes. Compared with polyolefin separators and liquid electrolytes, the safety and stability of batteries can be significantly improved in systems using polymer electrolyte because of their good electrochemical and thermal stability. Hence, many efforts have been made to develop advanced polymer electrolyte to boost battery performance. Kumar and Hashmi investigated ionic liquid/NaCF<sub>3</sub>SO<sub>3</sub> membranes gel polymer electrolyte toward sodium battery.<span><a href="#smll201601530-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201601530-bib-0027_R_d145161486e527" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Solid State Ionics">27</a></span> Unfortunately, too high viscosity of ionic liquid was not conducive to lithium ion conduction, which was detrimental to battery performance. In addition, the ultrahigh cost and unendurable viscosity of ionic liquids would restrict the industrial application. A freestanding membrane for sodium-ion conduction in PEGMA and BEMA polymer electrolyte (doped with NaClO<sub>4</sub>) has been developed via a UV-induced photopolymerization solution-casting technique by Bella et al.<span><a href="#smll201601530-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201601530-bib-0028_R_d145161486e531" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - ChemSusChem">28</a></span> Recently, Goodenough et al. explored a kind of low-cost glass-fiber/PVDF-HFP composite polymer electrolyte to improve battery performance.<span><a href="#smll201601530-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201601530-bib-0029_R_d145161486e533" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Adv. Energy Mater.">29</a></span> They found that such polymer electrolyte could improve battery performance to a certain degree, while the polymer electrolyte is too thick (200 μm) to enhance rate capability of sodium battery. Despite these great advances, we believe that it is still challenging to make attempts especially in new design concept to improve overall performance of polymer electrolyte for sodium battery.</p>
         
         <p>In the present study, we put forward a scalable and smart strategy to exploit a new class of polysulfonamide-supported poly(ethylene glycol) divinyl ether based polymer electrolyte (hereafter abbreviated as “PPDE-CPE”) for monolithic sodium ion battery by facile in situ integrated preparation. To date, this is the first article to use concept of in situ polymerization for fabricating polymer electrolyte of monolithic sodium ion full battery. It was demonstrated that the obtained polymer electrolyte possessed proper mechanical strength, wide electrochemical window, improved dimensional thermostability, and superior rate capability. In addition, the soft-package monolithic sodium ion full cell can still power a red light emitting diode lamp without suffering from internal short-circuit failures even in wide-angle bending or severely wrinkled condition. There is no denying that our study opens up a new kind of perspective that it is critical for fabricating high performance polymer electrolyte toward monolithic sodium ion battery.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll201601530-sec-0020">
         
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll201601530-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll201601530-sec-0030">
            
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="smll201601530-sec-0030-title">2.1 In Situ Preparation of PPDE-CPE</h3>
            
            <p>In situ synthesis process of PPDE-CPE was as follows: Homogeneous precursor solution was prepared by mixing 1 mL tri(ethylene glycol) divinyl ether (TEGDVE, Figure S1, Supporting Information), 2 mL organic electrolyte (1 <span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaClO<sub>4</sub> in propylene carbonate) and 0.01 g LiBF<sub>4</sub> (here, LiBF<sub>4</sub> acted as the initiator for the polymerization of TEGDVE). Subsequently, flame-retardant polysulfonamide (PSA) nonwoven was prepared via electrospinning process (Figure S2, Supporting Information).<span><a href="#smll201601530-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201601530-bib-0030_R_d145161486e565" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">30</a></span> After that, homogeneous precursor solution was then poured into a PSA nonwoven and the polymerization was conducted in vacuum oven at 25 °C for 5 h. Finally, a three dimensional framework structure filled by tri(ethylene glycol) divinyl ether based polymer electrolyte was formed in the pores of PSA nonwoven. The thickness of resultant polymer electrolyte membrane was 30 μm. They were stored under argon box for subsequent measurements. Compared with traditional process, such in situ preparation method greatly simplified the assembly process of sodium ion battery.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll201601530-sec-0040">
            
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="smll201601530-sec-0040-title">2.2 Morphology and Physical Properties</h3>
            
            <p>The typical scanning electron microscopy (SEM) images of PP separator, glass fiber, and PSA nonwoven were vividly shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll201601530-fig-0001">1</a>. It can be seen in Figure <a href="#smll201601530-fig-0001">1</a>a that the pores of PP separator are uniform and typically elliptic with the long axis of 100–300 nm and the short axis of about 50 nm. The elliptic shapes of the pores are the characteristic of the uniaxially stretched microporous membrane and it may give rise to thermal shutdown or shrinkage at the elevated temperature.<span><a href="#smll201601530-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201601530-bib-0021_R_d145161486e586" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - J. Solid State Electrochem.">21</a></span> Meanwhile, glass fiber (Figure <a href="#smll201601530-fig-0001">1</a>b) exhibited excessively large-sized pores between discontinuous fibers, which may lead to a decreased battery voltage due to self-discharge phenomenon.<span><a href="#smll201601530-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201601530-bib-0031_R_d145161486e591" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - J. Mater. Chem.">31</a></span> It was observed in Figure <a href="#smll201601530-fig-0001">1</a>c that PSA nonwoven was randomly consisted of numerous ultrafine nanofibers of 300 nm diameter with the pore size range of 0.5–1.5 μm. In addition, it also possessed tortuously labyrinth-like porous structure and with a thickness of about 30 μm (Figure S3, Supporting Information).</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="smll201601530-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/1b8ca8b5-d9b2-4744-8705-0124405786f8/smll201601530-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/1b8ca8b5-d9b2-4744-8705-0124405786f8/smll201601530-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/1e00de05-61f9-40fe-8d9a-94752c00bf8c/smll201601530-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/1b8ca8b5-d9b2-4744-8705-0124405786f8/smll201601530-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll201601530-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.201601530" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Typical SEM images of a) PP separator, b) glass fiber, and c) PSA nonwoven. d) Optical images of TEGDVE before and polymerization after storage at 25 °C for 6 h. e) Optical photograph and f) typical SEM image of PPDE-CPE. g) Optical photograph and typical SEM image of glass fiber after multiple bending. h) Thermal shrinkage ratio from 100 to 150 °C and i) stress–strain curves of PP separator, glass fiber and PPDE-CPE.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <p>Figure <a href="#smll201601530-fig-0001">1</a>d shows photographs of polymer electrolyte by in situ cationic polymerization of TEGDVE. FTIR analysis was conducted to confirm in situ cationic polymerization of divinyl ethers.<span><a href="#smll201601530-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201601530-bib-0032_R_d145161486e615" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Electrochem. Commun.">32</a></span> We found that the band at 1640 cm<sup>−1</sup> (CC) corresponding to vinyl functional group of TEGDVE disappeared after the completion of polymerization (Figure S4, Supporting Information), suggesting that homogeneous polymer electrolyte was obtained when the solution was stored at 25 °C for 5 h. An optical image of the PPDE-CPE film is presented in Figure <a href="#smll201601530-fig-0001">1</a>e, revealing the excellent flexibility of this polymer electrolyte. In addition, typical SEM image of the PPDE-CPE film and its (Energy Dispersive Spectroscopy) EDS images are also shown in Figure <a href="#smll201601530-fig-0001">1</a>f and Figure S5 of the Supporting Information, demonstrating the PSA-supported polymer electrolyte with continuous structure has been successfully obtained.</p>
            
            <p>It is well-known that the lower mechanical performance limits the application of separator or polymer electrolyte in energy storage devices.<span><a href="#smll201601530-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201601530-bib-0033_R_d145161486e627" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - J. Membr. Sci.">33</a></span> The stress–strain curves of PP separator, glass fiber, and PPDE-CPE were shown in Figure <a href="#smll201601530-fig-0001">1</a>g. Apparently, the mechanical strength of PPDE-CPE was enhanced to reach as high as 25 MPa, while the mechanical strength of PP separator and glass fiber was 10 and 2 MPa, respectively. Meanwhile, typical SEM image and digital photograph of glass fiber after repeated bended was shown in Figure <a href="#smll201601530-fig-0001">1</a>h. Apparently, fiber structure of glass fiber after repeated bending was badly broken. Hence, in terms of actual application, PPDE-CPE could deliver more reliable mechanical stability and subsequently improve the safety of batteries. Thermal shrinkage of separators is an essential factor to evaluate battery safety characteristics. A safe separator must be qualified to prevent the electrodes from contacting each other at elevated temperatures.<span><a href="#smll201601530-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201601530-bib-0026_R_d145161486e633" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - J. Power Sources">26</a></span> As shown in Figure <a href="#smll201601530-fig-0001">1</a>i, PPDE-CPE and glass fiber exhibited negligible thermal shrinkage over a wider range of temperatures, which could address the security issues caused by the leakage of organic electrolyte. On contrast, PP separator showed a high thermal shrinkage ratio (up to 50%) at elevated temperatures. Thus, the advisable comprehensive performances of microstructure, mechanical property, and thermal resistance of PPDE-CPE contribute to enhance the rate capability and cycling stability of sodium ion batteries.<span><a href="#smll201601530-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201601530-bib-0030_R_d145161486e638" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">30</a></span></p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll201601530-sec-0050">
            
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="smll201601530-sec-0050-title">2.3 Ionic Conductivity, Electrochemical Stability, and Interfacial Compatibility</h3>
            
            <p>It is crucial to evaluate the electrochemical stability of polymer electrolyte in the operating voltage for practical battery applications.<span><a href="#smll201601530-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201601530-bib-0034_R_d145161486e651" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - J. Power Sources">34</a></span> <b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll201601530-fig-0002">2</a>a shows the comparison of electrochemical window of various electrolyte system. When using different liquid electrolyte (1 <span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub> in EC/DMC; 1 <span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaClO<sub>4</sub> in EC/DMC; 1 <span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaClO<sub>4</sub> in PC), the electrochemical window of PP separator as well as glass fiber was stable up to 4.2, 4.4, and 4.5 V versus Na/Na<sup>+</sup>, respectively. In contrast, the anodic stability of PPDE-CPE can be further increased to 4.7 V versus Na/Na<sup>+</sup>. This superior electrochemical stability may be attributed to the better interfacial compatibility with electrodes.<span><a href="#smll201601530-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201601530-bib-0035_R_d145161486e676" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">35</a>, <a href="#smll201601530-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201601530-bib-0036_R_d145161486e676" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Solid State Ionics">36</a></span> Furthermore, we confirmed the better suitability of polyether-based polymer electrolyte (containing 1 <span class="smallCaps">m</span> LiODFB and 1 <span class="smallCaps">m</span> PC) through testing electrochemical performance of 4.5 V Li-ion cells with LiCoO<sub>2</sub> electrode (Figures S6 and S7, Supporting Information). Normal and stable charge and discharge profiles were observed in 4.5 V LiCoO<sub>2</sub>/Li cell. In addition, coulombic efficiency of the cell using polyether-based polymer electrolyte was about approximately 100%. Hence, polyether-based polymer electrolyte has an acceptable working voltage range for device applications including sodium batteries.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="smll201601530-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/e94c7678-9216-4fb5-aa3e-cd73b0dc9131/smll201601530-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/e94c7678-9216-4fb5-aa3e-cd73b0dc9131/smll201601530-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/c43c79ed-2a46-44a0-8286-2a96318214e5/smll201601530-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/e94c7678-9216-4fb5-aa3e-cd73b0dc9131/smll201601530-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll201601530-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.201601530" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) The comparison of electrochemical window of various electrolyte system. b) Ionic conductivity of PP separator, glass fiber, and PPDE-CPE as a function of temperature. c) Time–current curves following a direct current (DC) polarization of 10 mV for PP separator, glass fiber, and PPDE-CPE. d) Their Nyquist profiles of the cell electrochemical impedance spectroscopy response before and after DC polarization.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <div class="paragraph-element">Ionic conductivity is considered to be another key factor of electrolyte for its practical application. As is known, there are two behavior of ionic conductivity for electrolyte. The first is the Arrhenius equation. Another is described by the Vogel–Tamman–Fulcher (VTF) empirical equation

               <div class="inline-equation" id="smll201601530-disp-0001"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><img class="" src="/cms/asset/58fac8bf-4390-4754-9f24-8ada435de069/smll201601530-math-0001.png" alt="urn:x-wiley:dummy:media:smll201601530:smll201601530-math-0001" title="urn:x-wiley:dummy:media:smll201601530:smll201601530-math-0001" loading="lazy"></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(1)</span></div>
               
               <div class="inline-equation" id="smll201601530-disp-0002"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><img class="" src="/cms/asset/41d58dcb-032f-4d0a-a84c-270c717648dd/smll201601530-math-0002.png" alt="urn:x-wiley:dummy:media:smll201601530:smll201601530-math-0002" title="urn:x-wiley:dummy:media:smll201601530:smll201601530-math-0002" loading="lazy"></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(2)</span></div>
            </div>
            
            <p>where σ is ionic conductivity, <i>E</i><sub>a</sub> is the activation energy related to the segmental mobolity, <i>A</i> is the pre-exponential factor that depends on the material propert related to the charge carrier density, <i>R</i> is the ideal gas constant, <i>T</i><sub>0</sub> is the equilibrium glass transition temperature usually found to be 30–50 °C. Figure <a href="#smll201601530-fig-0002">2</a>b illustrated the relationship between log σ and 1000/<i>T</i>. It can be clearly seen that the log σ versus 1000/<i>T</i> for PP separator and glass fiber can be well fitted by linear relationships, indicating the temperature dependence of ionic conductivity for these two samples can be well modeled by Arrhenius equation. In comparison, a nonlinear best-fit is used to describe PPDE-CPE by VTF equation. In addition, the corresponding values of ionic conductivity, <i>E</i><sub>a</sub> and t<sub>Na+</sub> for three samples are also listed in <b>Table</b> <a class="tableLink scrollableLink" title="Link to table" href="#smll201601530-tbl-0001">1</a>. It is worth noticing that the <i>E</i><sub>a</sub> value of PPDE-CPE (6.4 KJ mol<sup>−1</sup>) is little lower than that for liquid electrolyte with PP separator (7.6 KJ mol<sup>−1</sup>), demonstrating that the movement of ions in PPDE-CPE needs a relatively lower energy as that in liquid electrolyte with PP separator. As shown in Figure S8 of the Supporting Information and Table <a class="tableLink scrollableLink" title="Link to table" href="#smll201601530-tbl-0001">1</a>, the ionic conductivity of PP separator, glass fiber and PPDE-SPE of 0.36, 2.6, and 1.2 mS cm<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. The ionic conductivity of PPDE-CPE is lower than that of glass fiber. However, taking into account other factors, such as mechanical property, electrochemical stability, PPDE-CPE would be more beneficial to the performance of the cells.</p>
            
            <div class="article-table-content" id="smll201601530-tbl-0001">
               <header class="article-table-caption"><span class="table-caption__label">Table 1.
                    </span>The values of ionic conductivity, <i>E</i><sub>a</sub> and t<sub>Na+</sub> of PP separator, glass fiber, and PPDE-CPE at 25 °C</header>
               
               
               
               <div class="article-table-content-wrapper" tabindex="0">
                  <table class="table article-section__table">
                     
                     
                     
                     
                     
                     <thead>
                        
                        <tr>
                           
                           <th class="bottom-bordered-cell right-bordered-cell left-aligned">Samples</th>

                     <tbody>

                           <td class="center-aligned">0.29</td>

                           <td class="center-aligned">0.46</td>
                           </tr>
                        </tbody>
                     </table>
               </div>
               
               
               <div class="article-section__table-source"></div>
            </div>
            
            <p>In general, both the cationic and anionic motion contributes significantly to the total ionic transport in the polymer electrolytes. Hence, cationic transport number measurement is a key parameter to evaluate the performance of the electrolytes from their application point of view in rechargeable batteries. In the present case, the transport number (t<sub>Na+</sub>) of sodium ions in the liquid electrolyte-soaked polymer electrolyte is obtained from a combination of AC impedance and DC polarization techniques.<span><a href="#smll201601530-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201601530-bib-0037_R_d145161486e857" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Electrochem. Commun.">37</a></span> In this technique, the Na/polymer electrolyte/Na cell was polarized at constant potential by applying a voltage, Δ<i>V</i> = 10 mV and the resultant current were monitored as a function of time. As shown in Figure <a href="#smll201601530-fig-0002">2</a>c,d and Table <a class="tableLink scrollableLink" title="Link to table" href="#smll201601530-tbl-0001">1</a>, the cationic transport number of liquid electrolyte-soaked PP separator, glass fiber and PPDE-CPE were 0.25, 0.29, and 0.46, respectively. It is obvious that t<sub>Na+</sub> for polymer electrolyte based on TEGDVE is much higher than that of PP separator and glass fiber. The reason could be ascribed to the polarity of TEGDVE, which restrains the movement of large ClO<sub>4</sub><sup>−</sup> anions. High Na<sup>+</sup> transference number of PPDE-SPE will be of significance to reduce the polarization during battery charging/discharging process.<span><a href="#smll201601530-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201601530-bib-0038_R_d145161486e873" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Solid State Ionics">38</a></span></p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll201601530-sec-0060">
            
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="smll201601530-sec-0060-title">2.4 Electrochemical Performance Evaluation of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>/Na Half Cell and MoS<sub>2</sub>/Na Half Battery at 25 °C</h3>
            
            <p>The SEM image, shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll201601530-fig-0003">3</a>a, reveals that the as-prepared Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> consists of agglomerates of primary particles having a size of about 40–60 nm. Figure <a href="#smll201601530-fig-0003">3</a>b presents the X-ray diffraction (XRD) patterns of the resultant Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> sample. All of the diffraction peaks are indexed to the R-3c space group and in good agreement with that reported in the literature.<span><a href="#smll201601530-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201601530-bib-0039_R_d145161486e922" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Adv. Energy Mater.">39</a></span> We further examined the rate capability of the Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>/Na cells using PP separator, glass fiber and PPDE-CPE. As shown in Figure <a href="#smll201601530-fig-0003">3</a>c, the specific discharge capacities of the Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>/Na cell using PPDE-CPE were 100.7, 100, 99.2, 98, 95.8, 93.7, 86.6, 78.8, and 67.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> correspondingly at varied rates of 0.2, 0.5, 1, 2, 3, 4, 6, 8, and 10 C, which were much better than those of PP separator. Inferior rate capability of PP separator can be attributed to its lyophobic characteristics and poor wettability with carbonate electrolyte. In comparison with glass fiber, discharge capacities of the cell using PPDE-CPE were similar to that of glass fiber at low rates (0.2, 0.5, 1, 2 C). However, when at relatively high rate (3, 4, 6, 8, and 10 C), rate performance of PPDE-CPE based battery was worse than that of battery using glass fiber. The difference in rate capability could be ascribed to the ionic conductivity from the different electrolyte.<span><a href="#smll201601530-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201601530-bib-0027_R_d145161486e945" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Solid State Ionics">27</a>-<a href="#smll201601530-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201601530-bib-0029_R_d145161486e945" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Adv. Energy Mater.">29</a></span> In addition, to further investigate the relationship between power density and energy density of polymer Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>/Na batteries quantitatively, we detected Ragone plot (Figure S9, Supporting Information) of polymer sodium battery using PPDE-CPE. It was interesting that the polymer sodium cells employing such polymer electrolyte can still deliver excellent rate capability and relatively high energy density at 20 °C, further proving its practicability and reliability using PPDE-CPE. As mentioned above, the thickness of glass fiber was 200 μm, which is too thick to promote overall performance of the battery. Taking into account other critical factors, such as physical property, electrochemical and interfacial stability, PPDE-CPE would be more beneficial to the performance of the cells.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="smll201601530-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/8f6aac4e-eac9-4edc-8d08-47a34d0ed31f/smll201601530-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/8f6aac4e-eac9-4edc-8d08-47a34d0ed31f/smll201601530-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/38875edc-dfa7-408d-97ec-2c85685b2acb/smll201601530-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/8f6aac4e-eac9-4edc-8d08-47a34d0ed31f/smll201601530-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll201601530-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.201601530" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Typical SEM image and b) XRD pattern of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> sample. c) Rate capability of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>/Na half cells using PP separator, glass fiber and PPDE-CPE, respectively. d) The charge/discharge profiles of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>/Na half cell using PPDE-CPE at varied current rates. e) Cycle performance of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>/Na half cells using PP separator, glass fiber, and PPDE-CPE, respectively. f) Impedance spectrum for the half cell using PP separator, glass fiber, and PPDE-CPE after 300 cycles.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <p>Figure <a href="#smll201601530-fig-0003">3</a>e showed cycle performances of half cells using PP separator, glass fiber and PPDE-SPE up to 300 cycle number, where glass fiber and PPDE-CPE was set at 3 C, PP separator was set at 0.2 C due to its low discharged specific capacity at 3 C. After 300 cycles, the discharged capacity retention of PPDE-CPE was 97.7% without distinct losses, which was much higher than that of glass fiber (92.9%) and PP separator (80%). In order to test self-discharge phenomenon, the ability of voltage retention, we detect the vibration of open-circuit voltage of the batteries after fully charged using glass fiber and PPDE-CPE. As can be seen from Figure S10 of the Supporting Information, the open-circuit voltage of glass fiber and PPDE-CPE were finally leveling out at around 3.39 V. Although glass fiber based cells had an excellent rate performance and cycle performance, the open-circuit voltage of this kind of cells would reduce sharply after 25 h when they were at idle state, which could be attributed to self discharge phenomenon. By comparison, PPDE-CPE had a better voltage retention performance for a long time storage.</p>
            
            <p>To achieve more comprehensive understanding for better cycling performance of the half cells, the interfacial resistance after the 300th cycle process was tracked in Figure <a href="#smll201601530-fig-0003">3</a>f. The bulk resistance of impedance spectrum is obtained from the intercept on the real-axis at high frequency, and the interfacial resistance calculated from the difference between the intercept at low frequency and bulk resistance, that is the semicircle in the impedance spectrum represents the interfacial resistance.<span><a href="#smll201601530-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201601530-bib-0023_R_d145161486e1017" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - J. Power Sources">23</a>, <a href="#smll201601530-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201601530-bib-0024_R_d145161486e1017" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - J. Power Sources">24</a></span> The interfacial resistance of the cells containing PPDE-CPE was 400 Ω after 300 cycles. While the cells containing glass fiber and PP separator were 550 and 1100 Ω, respectively. This result was consistent with the cycle performances of half cells using different separators or polymer electrolyte.<span><a href="#smll201601530-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201601530-bib-0031_R_d145161486e1019" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - J. Mater. Chem.">31</a></span></p>
            
            <p>The morphology of MoS<sub>2</sub> was observed by SEM imaging, as shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll201601530-fig-0004">4</a>a. The crystal structure of MoS<sub>2</sub> was characterized using XRD as shown in Figure <a href="#smll201601530-fig-0004">4</a>b. The diffraction profiles demonstrate the phase purity of MoS<sub>2</sub> with crystallized hexagonal structure (JCPDS 65–0160).<span><a href="#smll201601530-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201601530-bib-0040_R_d145161486e1037" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - J. Mater. Chem.">40</a></span> As shown in Figure <a href="#smll201601530-fig-0004">4</a>c, MoS<sub>2</sub>/Na cell using PPDE-CPE also exhibits good high rate capability. Even at a high current density of 10 C (discharge/charge time: &lt;6 min), discharge capacity was as high as 90 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>. High reversible capacities of 155 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.2 C, 150 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.5 C, 140 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 1 C, 135 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 2 C, 130 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 3 C, 125 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 4 C, 118 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 6 C, 110 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 8 C, and 90 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 10 C could be achieved, demonstrating outstanding high-rate performances of MoS<sub>2</sub>/Na cell using PPDE-SPE. Continuous cycling of MoS<sub>2</sub>/Na cell using PPDE-CPE for 50 cycles was carried out at 0.5 C (Figure <a href="#smll201601530-fig-0004">4</a>d), The stable capacity delivery of nearly 100% coulombic efficiency, indicated the excellent cycling stability of MoS<sub>2</sub>/Na cell using PPDE-CPE.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="smll201601530-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/55ac913c-8e96-4946-9dc7-180f9936191a/smll201601530-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/55ac913c-8e96-4946-9dc7-180f9936191a/smll201601530-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/7cf93bc8-a8e3-48fa-97da-da436d564309/smll201601530-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/55ac913c-8e96-4946-9dc7-180f9936191a/smll201601530-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll201601530-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.201601530" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Typical SEM image and b) XRD pattern of MoS<sub>2</sub> sample, c) rate capability of MoS<sub>2</sub>/Na cell at rates varying from 0.2 to 10 C at room temperature. The inset is charge/discharge profiles. d) Its cycle performance and coulomb efficiency at 0.5 C. (1 C = 117 mA g<sup>−1</sup>.)</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll201601530-sec-0070">
            
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="smll201601530-sec-0070-title">2.5 Battery Performance and Safety Evaluation of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>/MoS<sub>2</sub> Monolithic Sodium-Ion Full Battery</h3>
            
            <p>To validate the practical feasibility of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> and MoS<sub>2</sub> in a sodium metal free battery, monolithic Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>/MoS<sub>2</sub> sodium ion full cells were assembled (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll201601530-fig-0005">5</a>a) and measured between 1 and 3.6 V at the varied rates (Figure <a href="#smll201601530-fig-0005">5</a>b,c).PPDE-CPE based sodium ion full battery exhibited specific capacities of 91 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.2 C, 88 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.5 C, 83 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 1 C, 81 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 2 C, 79 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 4 C, 74 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 6 C, and 70 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 10 C, respectively. In addition, sodium ion full battery delivers a reversible capacity of 87 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.5 C and high capacity retention of 84% after 1000 cycles (Figure <a href="#smll201601530-fig-0005">5</a>d), indicating the excellent cyclability of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO4)<sub>3</sub>/MoS<sub>2</sub> sodium-ion full cell employing such polymer electrolyte.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="smll201601530-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/8b763382-eb33-401e-93b7-41c35ce689f3/smll201601530-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/8b763382-eb33-401e-93b7-41c35ce689f3/smll201601530-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/f1e34daa-779b-4141-a4d1-013f1ab6fee3/smll201601530-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/8b763382-eb33-401e-93b7-41c35ce689f3/smll201601530-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll201601530-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.201601530" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Flexible Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>/MoS<sub>2</sub> monolithic sodium-ion full cell. a) Schematic illustration for the fabrication of the monolithic sodium-ion full cell. b) Typical cross-section SEM image of monolithic sodium-ion full cell. c) Rate capability and d) charge/discharge profiles of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>/MoS<sub>2</sub> sodium-ion full battery at varied rates over the potential range of 1–3.6 V. e) Long-term cycling performance and its corresponding coulombic efficiency of the sodium-ion full cell at 0.5 C.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <p>Safety performance and flexibility of soft package battery is significant for the application of large-size battery. However, recently many accidents including thermal runaway and explosion result from the existence of thermally shrinkable separator and combustible organic gases.<span><a href="#smll201601530-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201601530-bib-0030_R_d145161486e1214" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">30</a></span> So the electrolyte system should exhibits very good safety characteristic including better thermal stability. As above-mentioned glass fiber was unable to realize the coiling process because of the poor mechanical strength. While the flexible PPDE-SPE was suitable for the soft packaged battery are assembled by coiling process, avoiding short-circuit, and thermal runaway.</p>
            
            <p>Finally, in order to explore the reliability of PPDE-SPE to flexible sodium-ion full cell with shape versatility, soft package cells comprising Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (as cathode), MoS<sub>2</sub> (as anode), and PPDE-CPE were developed and their performance was evaluated as a function of mechanical deformation. To demonstrate its potential application, the as-fabricated flexible full cell was used to power a commercial red light-emitting diode (LED). <b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll201601530-fig-0006">6</a>a shows that, under a free-bending condition, the cell is successfully able to light up a red LED lamp. As demonstrated in Figure <a href="#smll201601530-fig-0006">6</a>b and Video S1 of the Supporting Information, a LED can also be powered even when the battery was bended. No electrical failure was observed during the process of battery. Figure <a href="#smll201601530-fig-0006">6</a>c displayed that the red LED lamp is still running even after the cell is severely wrinkled. We also provided charge/discharge profiles of the cell under this harsh condition (Figure <a href="#smll201601530-fig-0006">6</a>d). It is worth mentioning that PPDE-CPE based sodium ion battery can still charge and discharge for finite number of times. Meanwhile, the cell exhibits very stable charge/discharge curve with cycling (up to 20 cycles). This is a solid evidence to prove that Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>/MoS<sub>2</sub> soft package sodium-ion full cell using PPDE-CPE shows unprecedented improvements in deformability and internal short-circuit prevention of cells. It is also expected that this monolithic Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>/MoS<sub>2</sub> sodium-ion full cell offers great promise as bendable electrodes in other energy-storage devices, smart electronics, and wearable devices.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="smll201601530-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/c1825321-f781-4780-aeb3-f248b885c43b/smll201601530-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/c1825321-f781-4780-aeb3-f248b885c43b/smll201601530-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/7b0e06ef-fea3-43ee-b365-61dfb0a399fe/smll201601530-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/c1825321-f781-4780-aeb3-f248b885c43b/smll201601530-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll201601530-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.201601530" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Optical photographs for the soft package Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>/MoS<sub>2</sub> sodium-ion full cell with PPDE-CPE that powers a red LED: a) Free-bending, b) bending, and c) wrinkled. d) Charge/discharge curves of the wrinkled Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>/MoS<sub>2</sub> soft package sodium-ion full cell after the first and the 20 cycle test (charge/discharge current density = 0.5/0.5 C).</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <p>On the basis of that, this research will pave the way for designing and exploiting safety-reinforced and wider electrochemical window poly(ethylene glycol) divinyl ether based polymer electrolyte for high-performance sodium ion batteries.</p>
            </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll201601530-sec-0080">
         
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll201601530-sec-0080-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>For the first time, polysulfonamide-supported poly(ethylene glycol) divinyl ether based polymer electrolyte was designed and fabricated toward monolithic sodium ion full battery through in situ polymerization process. As compared to liquid electrolyte, our polymer electrolyte exhibited comprehensive performance in terms of relatively higher ionic conductivity, wider electrochemical window and better flexibility. When evaluated as polymer electrolyte for rechargeable monolithic sodium ion full battery, such polymer electrolyte delivered a reversible capacity as high as 70 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> even at 10 C. In addition, the capacity retention of 84% over 1000 cycles was achieved for Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>/MoS<sub>2</sub> sodium ion full batteries. To our knowledge, this is the best rate capability and cycling stability of polymer electrolyte for sodium ion full battery. More impressively, such polymer sodium ion full battery demonstrated excellent safety performance and outstanding flexibility even in some harsh condition. We believe this simple and scalable in situ preparation method as well as the promising electrochemical performance of PPDE-SPE will provide new impetus to explore the practical application of polymer electrolyte for monolithic sodium ion full battery.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll201601530-sec-0090">
         
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll201601530-sec-0090-title">4 Experimental Section</h2>
         
         <p><i>Sample Collection</i>: Bulk molybdenum disulfide (MoS<sub>2</sub>) was purchased from Alfa Aesar. Sodium vanadium phosphate [Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>] was synthesized in our laboratory. Glass fiber (thickness: 200 μm) was obtained from Whatman Company. Commercialized microporous polypropylene separator was purchased from Celgard Company (USA).</p>
         
         <p><i>Samples Characterization</i>: A field emission scanning electron microscope (Hitachi S-4800, operating at 3 kV) was used to observe morphology of the samples. The FTIR spectra of the membranes are obtained using the attenuated total reflection (ATR) attachment on a Frontier FTIR spectrometer (Perkin-Elmer) in the transmission mode from 800 to 4000 cm<sup>−1</sup>, which is collected with 32 scans and a resolution of 4 cm<sup>−1</sup>. Thermal shrinkage ratio (TSR) was calculated using the equation (3): TSR (%) = [(<i>S</i><sub>0</sub>–<i>S</i><sub>1</sub>)/<i>S</i><sub>0</sub>] × 100%. <i>S</i><sub>0</sub> and <i>S</i><sub>1</sub> represent the area of the separator before and after thermal treatment at the varied temperature for 2 h, respectively. The stress–strain curves were measured using an Inston-3300 universal testing machine (USA) at a stretching speed of 1.66 mm s<sup>−1</sup> with the sample straps of 1 cm wide and 8 cm long.</p>
         
         <p><i>Electrochemical Evaluation</i>: The electrochemical stability of the separator was conducted by a linear sweep voltammetry (LSV). The liquid electrolyte-wetted separator was sandwiched between stainless-steel working electrode and Na metal reference electrode with 10 mV s<sup>−1</sup> scan rate from 0 V to 5.5 V versus Na<sup>+</sup>/Na. Ionic conductivity of separators was measured by preparing cells were sandwiched between stainless steel blocking electrodes (diameter: 16.2 mm). The measurements were performed using Zahner Zennium Electrochemical Workstation over a frequency range from 1 Hz to 10<sup>6</sup> Hz at 25 °C. The ionic conductivity (σ) is calculated by employing the equation (4): σ = <i>L</i>/<i>AR</i><sub>b</sub>, where <i>L</i> is the thickness of the separator sample, <i>A</i> is the contact area between the separator and the electrode, and <i>R</i><sub>b</sub> is the bulk impedance. The cation transference number t<sub>Na+</sub> was evaluated according to the combined complex impedance and DC polarization measurement technique. For this, the samples were sandwiched between a pair of sodium metal electrodes under argon. After having measured the total initial resistance by AC impedance in the frequency ranging from 1 Hz to 1 MHz, a DC potential of 10 mV was applied until a steady state was reached. Finally, the resistance was again measured by AC impedance. The cationic transference number has been obtained using the equation: (5) t<sub>Na+</sub> = <i>I</i><sub>s</sub> (∆<i>V</i>–<i>I</i><sub>0</sub><i>R</i><sub>0</sub>)/I<sub>0</sub> (∆<i>V</i>–<i>I</i><sub>s</sub><i>R</i><sub>s</sub>), where t<sub>Na+</sub> is the cationic transference number, ∆<i>V</i> is the potential applied across the cell, <i>I</i><sub>0</sub> and <i>I</i><sub>S</sub> are the initial and steady-state currents, and <i>R</i><sub>0</sub> and <i>R</i><sub>s</sub> are the cell resistances, evaluated using impedance measurements across the cells before and after their DC polarization.</p>
         
         <p>Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub> (PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> cathode was prepared by a doctor-blading and the mass ratio of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub> (PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>/Super P/LA133 was 8/1/1 (w/w/w). Rate capability and cycle life of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub> (PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>/Na half battery tests were carried out using a LAND battery testing system in the voltage range of 2.5–4.0 V at room temperature. The battery performance was evaluated under a constant charge/discharge current densities form 0.2 to 10 C for rate capability and 1 C for cycle performance test. The coin cell and aluminum soft packaged battery was assembled. Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub> (PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> is used as cathode, molybdenum disulfide (MoS<sub>2</sub>) is used as anode, which was prepared by a doctor-blading and the mass ratio of MoS<sub>2</sub>/Super P/LA133 was 8/1/1 (w/w/w). The initial charge–discharge curves and cycle performance were carried out using LAND battery testing system between 1 and 3.6 V at different rates. The C-rates in all of the electrochemical measurements are defined based on 1 C = 117 mA g<sup>−1</sup>.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">

                  <li data-bib-id="smll201601530-bib-0005"><span class="bullet">5</span><span class="author">H. Li</span>, <span class="author">Z. X. Wang</span>, <span class="author">L. Q.
